        <title>New reference genome sequences of hot pepper reveal the massive evolution of plant disease-resistance genes by retroduplication | Genome Biology | Full Text</title>

        

    <meta name="citation_abstract" content="Transposable elements are major evolutionary forces which can cause new genome structure and species diversification. The role of transposable elements in the expansion of nucleotide-binding and leucine-rich-repeat proteins (NLRs), the major disease-resistance gene families, has been unexplored in plants. We report two high-quality de novo genomes (Capsicum baccatum and C. chinense) and an improved reference genome (C. annuum) for peppers. Dynamic genome rearrangements involving translocations among chromosomes 3, 5, and 9 were detected in comparison between C. baccatum and the two other peppers. The amplification of athila LTR-retrotransposons, members of the gypsy superfamily, led to genome expansion in C. baccatum. In-depth genome-wide comparison of genes and repeats unveiled that the copy numbers of NLRs were greatly increased by LTR-retrotransposon-mediated retroduplication. Moreover, retroduplicated NLRs are abundant across the angiosperms and, in most cases, are lineage-specific. Our study reveals that retroduplication has played key roles for the massive emergence of NLR genes including functional disease-resistance genes in pepper plants."/>

        <meta property="og:type" content="article"/>
        <meta property="og:site_name" content="Genome Biology"/>
        <meta property="og:title" content="New reference genome sequences of hot pepper reveal the massive evolution of plant disease-resistance genes by retroduplication"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="Transposable elements are major evolutionary forces which can cause new genome structure and species diversification. The role of transposable elements in the expansion of nucleotide-binding and leucine-rich-repeat proteins (NLRs), the major disease-resistance gene families, has been unexplored in plants. We report two high-quality de novo genomes (Capsicum baccatum and C. chinense) and an improved reference genome (C. annuum) for peppers. Dynamic genome rearrangements involving translocations among chromosomes 3, 5, and 9 were detected in comparison between C. baccatum and the two other peppers. The amplification of athila LTR-retrotransposons, members of the gypsy superfamily, led to genome expansion in C. baccatum. In-depth genome-wide comparison of genes and repeats unveiled that the copy numbers of NLRs were greatly increased by LTR-retrotransposon-mediated retroduplication. Moreover, retroduplicated NLRs are abundant across the angiosperms and, in most cases, are lineage-specific. Our study reveals that retroduplication has played key roles for the massive emergence of NLR genes including functional disease-resistance genes in pepper plants."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/w200/springer-static/cover/journal/13059.jpg"/>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Background</h3><p>Transposable elements are major evolutionary forces which can cause new genome structure and species diversification. The role of transposable elements in the expansion of nucleotide-binding and leucine-rich-repeat proteins (NLRs), the major disease-resistance gene families, has been unexplored in plants.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Results</h3><p>We report two high-quality de novo genomes (<i>Capsicum baccatum</i> and <i>C. chinense</i>) and an improved reference genome (<i>C. annuum</i>) for peppers. Dynamic genome rearrangements involving translocations among chromosomes 3, 5, and 9 were detected in comparison between <i>C. baccatum</i> and the two other peppers. The amplification of <i>athila</i> LTR-retrotransposons, members of the <i>gypsy</i> superfamily, led to genome expansion in <i>C. baccatum</i>. In-depth genome-wide comparison of genes and repeats unveiled that the copy numbers of NLRs were greatly increased by LTR-retrotransposon-mediated retroduplication. Moreover, retroduplicated NLRs are abundant across the angiosperms and, in most cases, are lineage-specific.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Conclusions</h3><p>Our study reveals that retroduplication has played key roles for the massive emergence of NLR genes including functional disease-resistance genes in pepper plants.</p></div></div></section>
                    
    


                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Long terminal repeat retrotransposons (LTR-Rs) are a major evolutionary force in animals, fungi, and, especially, plants. They comprise &gt; 75% of many plant genomes and cause genomic instability, including genome expansion by amplification using an RNA intermediate [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bennetzen JL. Patterns in grass genome evolution. Curr Opin Plant Biol. 2007;10:176–81." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d9587e965">1</a>]. Besides genome expansion, LTR-Rs facilitate the creation of new candidate genes called retrogenes by means of retroduplication, in which spliced messenger RNA is captured, reverse transcribed, and subsequently integrated into the genome by a retrotransposon [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kaessmann H, Vinckenbosch N, Long M. RNA-based gene duplication: mechanistic and evolutionary insights. Nat Rev Genet. 2009;10:19–31." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d9587e968">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang W, Zheng H, Fan C, Li J, Shi J, Cai Z, et al. High rate of chimeric gene origination by retroposition in plant genomes. Plant Cell. 2006;18:1791–802." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d9587e968_1">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Zhu Z, Tan S, Zhang Y, Zhang YE. LINE-1-like retrotransposons contribute to RNA-based gene duplication in dicots. Sci Rep. 2016;6:24755." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d9587e971">4</a>]. In contrast to transduplication caused by class II transposable elements (TEs) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Jiang N, Bao ZR, Zhang XY, Eddy SR, Wessler SR. Pack-MULE transposable elements mediate gene evolution in plants. Nature. 2004;431:569–73." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d9587e974">5</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Morgante M, Brunner S, Pea G, Fengler K, Zuccolo A, Rafalski A. Gene duplication and exon shuffling by helitron-like transposons generate intraspecies diversity in maize. Nat Genet. 2005;37:997–1002." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d9587e977">6</a>], the distinctive features of retrogenes are: (1) intron loss compared to their parental source genes; (2) the presence of a 3′ poly(A) tail; and (3) flanking direct repeats [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Ohshima K. RNA-mediated gene duplication and retroposons: retrogenes, LINEs, SINEs, and sequence specificity. Int J Evol Biol. 2013;2013:424726." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d9587e981">7</a>].</p><p>The evolutionary forces acting on most plant retrogenes are still largely unclear [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Wang W, Zheng H, Fan C, Li J, Shi J, Cai Z, et al. High rate of chimeric gene origination by retroposition in plant genomes. Plant Cell. 2006;18:1791–802." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d9587e987">3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lisch D. How important are transposons for plant evolution? Nat Rev Genet. 2013;14:49–61." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d9587e990">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sakai H, Mizuno H, Kawahara Y, Wakimoto H, Ikawa H, Kawahigashi H, et al. Retrogenes in rice (Oryza sativa L. ssp japonica) exhibit correlated expression with their source genes. Genome Biol Evol. 2011;3:1357–68." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d9587e990_1">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhang C, Gschwend AR, Ouyang Y, Long M. Evolution of gene structural complexity: an alternative-splicing-based model accounts for intron-containing retrogenes. Plant Physiol. 2014;165:412–23." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d9587e990_2">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Zhu Z, Zhang Y, Long M. Extensive structural renovation of retrogenes in the evolution of the Populus genome. Plant Physiol. 2009;151:1943–51." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d9587e993">11</a>]. Although LTR-Rs are the most abundant TEs in all but the tiniest plant genomes, few studies have been reported on the detection of retrogenes generated by LTR-Rs in plants [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Elrouby N, Bureau TE. A novel hybrid open reading frame formed by multiple cellular gene transductions by a plant long terminal repeat retroelement. J Biol Chem. 2001;276:41963–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d9587e996">12</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Jin YK, Bennetzen JL. Integration and nonrandom mutation of a plasma-membrane proton atpase gene fragment within the Bs1 retroelement of maize. Plant Cell. 1994;6:1177–86." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d9587e999">13</a>]. Wang et al. [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Wang W, Zheng H, Fan C, Li J, Shi J, Cai Z, et al. High rate of chimeric gene origination by retroposition in plant genomes. Plant Cell. 2006;18:1791–802." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1003">3</a>] identified 27 retrogenes within LTR-Rs in rice and concluded that retrogenes that originated within LTR-Rs were often not classified as retrogenes, partly because of the rapid destruction of the LTR-R structure by illegitimate recombination [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Ma J, Devos KM, Bennetzen JL. Analyses of LTR-retrotransposon structures reveal recent and rapid genomic DNA loss in rice. Genome Res. 2004;14:860–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1006">14</a>]. Moreover, they suggested that the retrogenes might be very frequent in grass species due to the abundance of LTR-Rs. In agreement with this prediction, recent studies have reported the genome-wide identification of hundreds of retrogenes within LTR-Rs in maize [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Baucom RS, Estill JC, Chaparro C, Upshaw N, Jogi A, Deragon JM, et al. Exceptional diversity, non-random distribution, and rapid evolution of retroelements in the B73 maize genome. PLoS Genet. 2009;5:e1000732." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1009">15</a>], rice, and sorghum [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Jiang SY, Ramachandran S. Genome-wide survey and comparative analysis of LTR retrotransposons and their captured genes in rice and sorghum. PLoS One. 2013;8:e71118." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1012">16</a>] as well as the existence of retrogenes captured by LTR-Rs in <i>Arabidopsis</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Zhu Z, Tan S, Zhang Y, Zhang YE. LINE-1-like retrotransposons contribute to RNA-based gene duplication in dicots. Sci Rep. 2016;6:24755." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1018">4</a>]. However, most of those retrogenes were classified as pseudogenes or uncharacterized genes.</p><p>Previous studies reported the massive capture of specific gene families by certain TEs and suggested a correlation between TE-mediated gene duplication and specific gene family expansion [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Hoen DR, Park KC, Elrouby N, Yu Z, Mohabir N, Cowan RK, et al. Transposon-mediated expansion and diversification of a family of ULP-like genes. Mol Biol Evol. 2006;23:1254–68." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1024">17</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Kong H, Landherr LL, Frohlich MW, Leebens-Mack J, Ma H, de Pamphilis CW. Patterns of gene duplication in the plant SKP1 gene family in angiosperms: evidence for multiple mechanisms of rapid gene birth. Plant J. 2007;50:873–85." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1027">18</a>]. The nucleotide-binding and leucine-rich-repeat proteins (NLRs) represent a highly amplified gene family in plants and provide the majority of functional plant disease resistance loci [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Guo YL, Fitz J, Schneeberger K, Ossowski S, Cao J, Weigel D. Genome-wide comparison of nucleotide-binding site-leucine-rich repeat-encoding genes in Arabidopsis. Plant Physiol. 2011;157:757–69." href="#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1030">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ratnaparkhe MB, Wang XY, Li JP, Compton RO, Rainville LK, Lemke C, et al. Comparative analysis of peanut NBS-LRR gene clusters suggests evolutionary innovation among duplicated domains and erosion of gene microsynteny. New Phytol. 2011;192:164–78." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1030_1">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Seo E, Kim S, Yeom SI, Choi D. Genome-wide comparative analyses reveal the dynamic evolution of nucleotide-binding leucine-rich repeat gene family among Solanaceae Plants. Front Plant Sci. 2016;7:1205." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1033">21</a>]. Comparative genomic analyses have suggested the possibility of LTR-Rs and NLRs co-evolution, partly because they are often co-localized [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Ratnaparkhe MB, Wang XY, Li JP, Compton RO, Rainville LK, Lemke C, et al. Comparative analysis of peanut NBS-LRR gene clusters suggests evolutionary innovation among duplicated domains and erosion of gene microsynteny. New Phytol. 2011;192:164–78." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1036">20</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Hayashi K, Yoshida H. Refunctionalization of the ancient rice blast disease resistance gene Pit by the recruitment of a retrotransposon as a promoter. Plant J. 2009;57:413–25." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1040">22</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Kuykendall D, Shao J, Trimmer K. A nest of LTR retrotransposons adjacent the disease resistance-priming gene NPR1 in Beta vulgaris L. U.S. Hybrid H20. Int J Plant Genomics. 2009;2009:576742." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1043">23</a>]. Because the NLRs usually reside in clusters within genomes, NLR expansions have been mainly interpreted as the products of ectopic recombinational duplications [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Guo YL, Fitz J, Schneeberger K, Ossowski S, Cao J, Weigel D. Genome-wide comparison of nucleotide-binding site-leucine-rich repeat-encoding genes in Arabidopsis. Plant Physiol. 2011;157:757–69." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1046">19</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Leister D. Tandem and segmental gene duplication and recombination in the evolution of plant disease resistance gene. Trends Genet. 2004;20:116–22." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1049">24</a>].</p><p>Here, we report high-quality de novo genome sequences of two domesticated <i>Capsicum</i> species and also improved the quality of the reference pepper genome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Kim S, Park M, Yeom SI, Kim YM, Lee JM, Lee HA, et al. Genome sequence of the hot pepper provides insights into the evolution of pungency in Capsicum species. Nat Genet. 2014;46:270–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1058">25</a>]. Comparative analyses of the three pepper genomes, with other plant genomes as outgroups, provided insights into genome evolution and species diversification in the genus <i>Capsicum</i>. Our analyses unveil an important mechanism for the massive emergence of new plant NLRs by LTR-R-mediated retroduplication and show the dynamic evolutionary processes for functional disease resistance genes in plants.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results and discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results and discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">De novo sequencing, assembly, and annotation of <i>Capsicum</i> genomes</h3><p>We sequenced and assembled the genome sequences of <i>Capsicum baccatum</i> PBC81 (hereafter, Baccatum) and <i>C. chinense</i> PI159236 (hereafter, Chinense) using Illumina HiSeq 2500 with library insert sizes in the range of 200 bp–10 kb (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S1-S3). The estimated genome sizes of Baccatum and Chinense, based on 19-mer analysis, were 3.9 and 3.2 Gb, respectively (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S1). The assembled genomes of Baccatum and Chinense constituted 3.2 and 3.0 Gb (83% and 94% of the estimated genome sizes, respectively) and had scaffold (contig) N50 sizes of 2.0 Mb (39 kb) and 3.3 Mb (50 kb), respectively (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S3). We annotated the protein-coding genes in the Baccatum and Chinense assemblies as well as those in the pre-existing <i>C. annuum</i> CM334 genome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Kim S, Park M, Yeom SI, Kim YM, Lee JM, Lee HA, et al. Genome sequence of the hot pepper provides insights into the evolution of pungency in Capsicum species. Nat Genet. 2014;46:270–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1099">25</a>] (hereafter, Annuum) for detailed comparative analysis (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S2). On average, ~ 35,000 genes were annotated in each species (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S4). Our analysis revealed higher gene coverage in the updated gene model than that in the previous gene model of Annuum (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S5). Furthermore, a comparison of the updated and previous gene models of Annuum revealed ~ 10,000 genes that did not overlap between the two gene models, suggesting that most of the non-overlapping genes in the previous version were associated with TEs (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S3).</p><p>A high-density genetic map of each species was generated by genotyping-by-sequencing on F2 populations [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Jeong HS, Jang S, Han K, Kwon JK, Kang BC. Marker-assisted backcross breeding for development of pepper varieties (Capsicum annuum) containing capsinoids. Mol Breed. 2015;35:226." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1117">26</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Lee YR, Yoon JB, Lee J. A SNP-based genetic linkage map of Capsicum baccatum and its comparison to the Capsicum annuum reference physical map. Mol Breed. 2016;36:61." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1120">27</a>]. After breaking up chimeric scaffolds on the basis of genetic map information, we organized the assembled genome sequences into 12 chromosomes-scale pseudomolecules. Overall, 87% of Baccatum (2.8 Gb in 2083 scaffolds) and 89% of Chinense (2.8 Gb in 1,557 scaffolds) in assembled genomes were ordered by the genetic map and inspected for syntenic inferences with the updated pseudomolecules of Annuum (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S6). We validated the assembled genome sequences by reference guided mapping using the refined single-end and paired-end data, and alignment to the de novo transcriptome assembly of each species (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S7 and S8). In total, we detected more than 98.1% of the filtered raw sequences (&gt;98% identity) and more than 93.4% of the assembled transcriptomes (&gt; 98% identity and 80% of query coverage) in the genome assemblies (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S8). Taken together, our analyses provide the de novo reference genome sequences of two new pepper species as well as an improved Annuum genome sequences.</p><p>Repeat annotation was performed with the assembled genomes and the initial contigs covering the estimated genome sizes of the three species (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S4, Table S9 and S10). Overall, ~ 85% of the initial contigs were annotated as repeat sequences. LTR-Rs of the Ty3-<i>gypsy</i> superfamily accounted for about half of the entire genome in each of the three species (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S9 and S10). Among the subgroups of the <i>gypsy</i> superfamily, <i>del</i> elements comprised the largest fraction, representing 41.5%, 34.9%, and 41.7% (1482, 1337, and 1,343 Mb) in Annuum, Baccatum and Chinense, respectively. Furthermore, <i>athila</i> elements were more abundant (&gt; 2-fold) in Baccatum, indicating that the <i>athila</i> subgroup contributed to species-specific genome expansion in the Baccatum lineage (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S10).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Speciation and evolution of the <i>Capsicum</i> species</h3><p>A phylogenetic analysis of the peppers with other plant species revealed that the divergence among the three peppers occurred first between Baccatum and a progenitor of the other two peppers ~ 1.7 million years ago (MYA), followed by divergence between Annuum and Chinense lineages ~1.1 MYA (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig1">1a</a>; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S11). To identify genomic changes in the three pepper species, we compared the genome structures, LTR-R insertion patterns, and gene duplication histories across these pepper genomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig1">1b, c</a> and Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig2">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-017-1341-9/MediaObjects/13059_2017_1341_Fig1_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-017-1341-9/MediaObjects/13059_2017_1341_Fig1_HTML.gif" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Lineage-divergence and genome structure comparisons of three <i>Capsicum</i> species. <b>a</b> The reconstructed <i>phylogenetic tree</i> of eight plant genomes indicates their evolutionary relationships and estimated divergence times. <b>b</b> The <i>circular diagram</i> shows the distribution of repeats, genes, genomic variations, and genome rearrangements in the pepper genomes. The subcategories indicate the density of repeats (A), genes (B), species-specific blocks (C), and SNPs (D) in the pepper genomes. The subcategory (E) depicts collinear and translocated blocks among the pepper genomes. <b>c</b> A linear comparison of the rearranged blocks in the pepper genomes. <i>Colors</i> in the <i>bars</i> indicate translocated regions when comparing to tomato and potato genomes. The <i>line colors</i> indicate translocations in the ancestral lineage leading to Annuum and Chinense (<i>red</i>), in Baccatum (<i>green</i>), and in the ancestor of Annuum and Chinense or Baccatum (<i>dark gray</i>)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                           <div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-017-1341-9/MediaObjects/13059_2017_1341_Fig2_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-017-1341-9/MediaObjects/13059_2017_1341_Fig2_HTML.gif" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Evolutionary history of LTR-Rs and duplications of protein-coding genes in the pepper genomes. <b>a</b> Distribution of LTR-R insertions. The <i>graphs</i> in the <i>left</i> and <i>right panels</i> depict the predicted insertion dates of LTR-R superfamilies (<i>gypsy</i>, <i>copia</i>) and two specific families (<i>del</i>, <i>athila</i>). The <i>x-</i> and <i>y-</i>axes indicate the insertion times and the number of insertions at each time, respectively. <b>b</b> Time-scaled gene duplication history (<i>left</i>) and top ten repertoires of massive gene duplication (<i>right</i>). The <i>x-</i> and <i>y-</i>axes of the graph in the <i>left panel</i> indicate the approximate duplication time (MYA) and the number of gene duplications, respectively. The <i>x-</i> and <i>y-</i>axes of the histogram in the <i>right panel</i> represent the number of genes and domain description, respectively</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>Chromosomal rearrangement is an important force in speciation, often producing unbalanced gametes that reduce hybrid fertility [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Rieseberg LH. Chromosomal rearrangements and speciation. Trends Ecol Evol. 2001;16:351–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1324">28</a>]. We performed an inter-genomic structural comparison and detected translocations among the pepper genomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig1">1b</a>). The results show that chromosomes 3, 5, and 9 exhibit translocations that differentiate Baccatum from the other two species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig1">1b, c</a>). Collinearity comparisons among <i>Capsicum</i> species and two <i>Solanum</i> species revealed that the distal region on the long arm of chromosome 9 was conserved in Baccatum but translocated to the short arm of chromosome 3 in a shared ancestor of Annuum and Chinense (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig1">1c</a>; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S5). Furthermore, chromosomes 6 and 4 of <i>Solanum</i> were detected in the terminal regions of the long and short arms of chromosomes 3 and 5 in Annuum and Chinense, respectively. In contrast, the orthologous regions of <i>Solanum</i> were mixed in the corresponding blocks of Baccatum (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig1">1c</a>). This indicates that the distal regions of the long and short arms of chromosomes 3 and 5 were translocated in the Baccatum lineage. We detected translocations between the terminal regions of the short arm of chromosome 3 in Baccatum and the long arm of chromosome 9 in Annuum and Chinense. Consequently, our analyses revealed that translocations have generated hetero karyotypes in both the Baccatum and the Annuum/Chinense progenitor lineages.</p><p>To compare LTR-R insertion patterns across the pepper genomes, we identified full-length LTR-Rs in each assembled genome and estimated their insertion times [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="SanMiguel P, Gaut BS, Tikhonov A, Nakajima Y, Bennetzen JL. The paleontology of intergene retrotransposons of maize. Nat Genet. 1998;20:43–5." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1358">29</a>] (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S5 and Table S12). A peak of LTR-R activity in Baccatum appeared around its speciation time 1–2 MYA (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig2">2a</a>). In particular, the <i>athila</i> family was highly amplified in Baccatum around the estimated speciation time, indicating that this subgroup may have explosively increased in Baccatum after speciation. In Chinense, we observed the recent proliferation of LTR-Rs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig2">2a</a>).</p><p>Gene duplication is a major mechanism generating functional diversity between species by the creation of new genes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Flagel LE, Wendel JF. Gene duplication and evolutionary novelty in plants. New Phytol. 2009;183:557–64." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1376">30</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Panchy N, Lehti-Shiu M, Shiu SH. Evolution of gene duplication in plants. Plant Physiol. 2016;171:2294–316." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1379">31</a>]. We detected recent gene duplication events and characterized the repertories of duplicated genes in the three pepper genomes during and after speciation (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig2">2b</a>). Overall, the duplication events were particularly frequent in the Baccatum lineage, both during and after the speciation. In particular, NLRs were extensively amplified in Baccatum in the last 0–2 MYA, and more recently in the other two peppers (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig2">2b</a>). Taken together, those results suggest that the chromosomal rearrangements, accumulation of specific LTR-Rs, and differential gene duplications have contributed to genome diversification in the <i>Capsicum</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Massive creation of new NLRs via LTR-R-driven retroduplication</h3><p>A previous study suggested that NLRs were amplified in pepper compared to tomato and potato genomes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Seo E, Kim S, Yeom SI, Choi D. Genome-wide comparative analyses reveal the dynamic evolution of nucleotide-binding leucine-rich repeat gene family among Solanaceae Plants. Front Plant Sci. 2016;7:1205." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1399">21</a>]. In particular, the coiled-coil NLR subgroup 2 (CNL-G2) was highly expanded in the pepper genome (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S13). To explore the possible mechanism of the NLR proliferation in <i>Capsicum</i> spp., we analyzed the NLRs and their flanking sequences. We identified 105, 123, and 86 NLRs located inside LTR-Rs in Annuum, Baccatum, and Chinense, respectively (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S6, S7, and Table S13; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S14). Hence, a large proportion (~13%) of the NLRs were amplified by LTR-Rs, with the structures indicating that their retroduplicated origin is still intact. The retroduplicated NLRs were manly located on specific euchromatic chromosome arms (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S8). Most of these NLRs (~70%) were in the CNL-G2 category, indicating that the copy number of specific NLR subfamilies was particularly expanded in specific chromosomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig3">3a, b</a>; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S8). Furthermore, most of the retroduplicated NLRs (~ 72% of the total and ~ 67% of the CNL-G2 type) were inside non-autonomous LTR-Rs that contained no <i>gag</i> or <i>pol</i> protein coding potential (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S15). This suggests that all steps for the retroduplication, presumably including the initial sequence capture process, had to be provided <i>in trans</i>. To compare retroduplicated NLRs and NLRs which are not affected by LTR-Rs, we classified normal NLRs as false-negative annotations (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S13). We performed <i>Ka/Ks</i> analysis to compare selection pressure between retroduplicated and normal NLRs in CNL-G2. Our analysis revealed that both retroduplicated and normal NLRs were under purifying selection and <i>Ka/Ks</i> ratio of the both genes was not significantly different (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S9).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-017-1341-9/MediaObjects/13059_2017_1341_Fig3_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-017-1341-9/MediaObjects/13059_2017_1341_Fig3_HTML.gif" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Emergence of large NLR gene families by retroduplication. <b>a</b> Intact NB-ARC domains of retroduplicated and normal NLRs are used for the <i>phylogenetic tree construction</i> as previously described method [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Seo E, Kim S, Yeom SI, Choi D. Genome-wide comparative analyses reveal the dynamic evolution of nucleotide-binding leucine-rich repeat gene family among Solanaceae Plants. Front Plant Sci. 2016;7:1205." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1465">21</a>]. Each <i>color</i> of the phylogenetic tree indicates each subgroup. <b>b</b> The <i>bar graph</i> indicates the number of retroduplicated NLRs in each subgroup. The <i>x-</i> and <i>y-</i>axes indicate the numbers of genes and subgroups, respectively. <b>c</b>, <b>d</b> The exon lengths and the numbers of normal and retroduplicated NLRs are depicted. <b>c</b> The <i>x-</i> and <i>y-</i>axes indicate the normal and retroduplicated NLR groups and their exon lengths, respectively. <b>d</b> The <i>x-</i> and <i>y-</i>axes mean the groups of NLRs and the exon numbers, respectively</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>When we compared the retroduplicated and normal NLRs in the CNL-G2 category, the number and length of exons were significantly fewer and longer in the retroduplicated NLRs, but not all of the retroduplicated NLRs have single exons (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig3">3c, d</a>). In total, ~ 32% of the retroduplicated NLRs in each species had multiple exons but all of those had a reduced number of introns compared to their predicted parental sequences (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S7b, Table S15 and S16). For example, CB.v1.2.scaffold1410.1 having two exons is annotated as a retroduplicated NLR in CNG-G10 of Baccatum. We found that its potential parental sequence was consisted of six exons and sequence comparison of the both genes revealed that five exons of parental sequence were fully merged to the first exon (2.9 kbp) of CB.v1.2.scaffold1410.1 (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S7b). These results suggest that retroduplicated NLRs containing introns in pepper genomes might be emerged through alternative splicing mechanisms such as intron retention or exon skipping as described in Zhang et al. [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Zhang C, Gschwend AR, Ouyang Y, Long M. Evolution of gene structural complexity: an alternative-splicing-based model accounts for intron-containing retrogenes. Plant Physiol. 2014;165:412–23." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1530">10</a>].</p><p>We performed the genome-wide analyses using tomato, potato, and rice genomes to verify that the retroduplication is a general feature of genome evolution in the plant kingdom (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S13). We found that 21, 81, and 27 (8%, 18%, and 5%) of NLRs were inside LTR-Rs in tomato, potato, and rice, respectively (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S13). Of these, we identified parental sequences with multiple exons for 14, 71, and 16 of the NLRs inside LTR-Rs in tomato, potato, and rice, respectively, thus confirming their emergence by retroduplication (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S10 and Table S17). Similar to the peppers, NLRs in a particular subgroup (CNL-G9) were primarily retroduplicated in potato (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S13). These results indicate that LTR-Rs played a key role in the expansion of NLRs by retroduplication throughout the plant kingdom and that the detected events are both recent and lineage-specific.</p><p>In addition to the NLRs, we looked for other genes inside LTR-Rs in the six plant species (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S18). In total, a range from 1398 genes in rice to 3898 genes in potato genomes were found to be inside LTR-Rs, suggesting possibility for emergence of a large proportion of genes in these plant species by LTR-R-driven duplication. On average, ~ 45% of them had functional domains including highly amplified families such as MADS-box TFs, cytochrome P450s, and protein kinases, and ~ 42% of those genes were expressed in one or more investigated tissues by RNA-sequencing (RNA-seq) analysis (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S18).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Evolutionary mechanisms for the emergence of disease resistance genes in Solanaceae</h3><p>The <i>L</i> genes encoded by the NLRs are known to provide resistance in peppers against <i>Tobamoviruses</i> and they belong to the CNL-G4 category, along with <i>I2</i> in tomato that provides resistance to race 2 of <i>Fusarium oxysporum</i> f. sp. <i>lycopersici</i> and <i>R3a</i> in potato that provides resistance to the late blight pathogen, <i>Phytophthora infestans</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Simons G, Groenendijk J, Wijbrandi J, Reijans M, Groenen J, Diergaarde P, et al. Dissection of the fusarium I2 gene cluster in tomato reveals six homologs and one active gene copy. Plant Cell. 1998;10:1055–68." href="#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1587">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Huang S, van der Vossen EA, Kuang H, Vleeshouwers VGAA, Zhang N, Borm TJ, et al. Comparative genomics enabled the isolation of the R3a late blight resistance gene in potato. Plant J. 2005;42:251–61." href="#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1587_1">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Tomita R, Sekine KT, Mizumoto H, Sakamoto M, Murai J, Kiba A, et al. Genetic basis for the hierarchical interaction between Tobamovirus spp. and L resistance gene alleles from different pepper species. Mol Plant Microbe Interact. 2011;24:108–17." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1590">34</a>]. Each gene has single exon encoding a peptide of ~ 1300 amino acids. Synteny analysis and sequence comparison among pepper, potato, and tomato genomes suggested <i>L</i>, <i>I2</i>, and <i>R3a</i> are orthologous genes and the genomic regions containing <i>L</i>, <i>I2</i>, and <i>R3a</i> were tightly linked on chromosome 11 (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S11a and Table S19). These results suggest the possibility that the genes originated by an early retroduplication and then underwent divergent evolution in each lineage.</p><p>We examined the evolutionary history of <i>L</i> genes and their putative parental genes in the pepper genomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig4">4</a>; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S11b, c, and Table S20). The candidates for a parental gene (P1 to P6) were identified considering similarity, <i>Ks</i> values, and alignment coverage to <i>L</i> genes. All candidate parental sequences contained multiple exons. When candidate parental sequence P1 was compared with <i>L</i> in Annuum, the results suggested that <i>L</i> was derived from retroduplication in the ancestral lineage of <i>Capsicum</i> spp. ~ 8.9 MYA (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig4">4</a>). Because <i>L</i> has a 6.7-kb single exon, with only an intron in the 3′ UTR, and the presence of both flanking direct repeat sequences and a poly(A) “tail,” our analysis suggests that <i>L</i> emerged through capture and reverse transcription by a long interspersed nuclear element (LINE)-driven retroduplication (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig4">4</a>; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S12). Sequence comparison of <i>L</i> genes in the three genomes and <i>L4</i> in <i>C. chacoense</i> revealed that the <i>L</i> genes were diversified by accumulation of lineage-specific sequence mutations after speciation within <i>Capsicum</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig4">4</a>; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S21). Consequently, our results suggest that the ancestor of the <i>L</i> genes was derived from retroduplication and that subsequent divergent evolution has led to specific resistance against diverse strains of <i>Tobamovirus</i> in each species of <i>Capsicum</i> after speciation (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig4">4</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-017-1341-9/MediaObjects/13059_2017_1341_Fig4_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-017-1341-9/MediaObjects/13059_2017_1341_Fig4_HTML.gif" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Emergence and evolution of <i>L</i> and <i>R3a</i> genes in the pepper and potato genomes. Models for the evolution of <i>L</i> and <i>R3a</i> in the pepper and potato are depicted. The gene names in the <i>R3a</i> cluster are from the previous analysis of Huang et al. [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Huang S, van der Vossen EA, Kuang H, Vleeshouwers VGAA, Zhang N, Borm TJ, et al. Comparative genomics enabled the isolation of the R3a late blight resistance gene in potato. Plant J. 2005;42:251–61." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1720">33</a>]. The model proposes that <i>L</i> and <i>R3a</i> gene ancestors were first created by retroduplication, followed by the accumulation of point mutations and tandem duplication, respectively. DNA sequence indicative of a poly(A) tail and flanking terminal repeat (TR) sequences are depicted in the <i>diagram</i> as genomic evidence for a retroduplicated origin of <i>L</i>
                                    </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>To analyze the evolutionary processes acting on <i>R3a</i> of potato, we first performed a genome-wide search for the <i>R3a</i> as well as for candidate parental sequences. Because <i>R3a</i> is absent in the current potato reference genome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature. 2011;475:189–95." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1756">35</a>], we could not carry out accurate comparisons of <i>R3a</i> and their homologs. However, <i>R3a</i> and its clustered genes originated from wild species, <i>Solanum demissum</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Huang S, Vleeshouwers VGAA, Werij JS, Hutten RCB, van Eck HJ, Visser RGF, et al. The R3 resistance to Phytophthora infestans in potato is conferred by two closely linked R genes with distinct specificities. Mol Plant Microbe Interact. 2004;17:428–35." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1769">36</a>], and were available in a public database. So, we compared these sequences with their closest homologs in the reference potato genome. Our analyses revealed that intronless sequences of the ancestral potato <i>R3a</i> might have emerged by RNA-based gene duplication in a shared ancestor of potato and tomato (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig4">4</a>). Subsequently, <i>R3a</i> and its paralogues were amplified by two rounds of tandem gene duplication after the divergence of potato and tomato (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig4">4</a>; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S22). Taken together, our results suggest that retroduplication events are a main evolutionary process in the emergence of new plant disease resistance genes, which can gain function via subsequent sequence variation and tandem duplication.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Evolution of potential anthracnose resistance genes in Baccatum</h3><p>Pepper anthracnose caused by <i>Colletotrichum</i> spp. is one of the most devastating diseases in worldwide pepper production [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Than PP, Prihastuti H, Phoulivong S, Taylor PW, Hyde KD. Chilli anthracnose disease caused by Colletotrichum species. J Zhejiang Univ Sci B. 2008;9:764–78." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1800">37</a>]. Due to the complexity of the interactions between the host and <i>Colletotrichum</i> spp. and the lack of resistance in the Annuum gene pool, a few Baccatum varieties were identified as the only breeding resources for anthracnose resistance [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Mahasuk P, Struss D, Mongkolporn O. QTLs for resistance to anthracnose identified in two Capsicum sources. Mol Breed. 2016;36:10." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1806">38</a>]. Using pre-existing genetic information [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Lee J, Do JW, Yoon JB. Development of STS markers linked to the major QTLs for resistance to the pepper anthracnose caused by Colletotrichum acutatum and C. capsici. Hortic Environ Biotechnol. 2011;52:596–601." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1809">39</a>], we identified the pertinent genomic regions and obtained 64 NLRs from a 3.8 Mb region of Baccatum chromosome 3 as candidate resistance genes for <i>C. capsici</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig5">5</a>; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S23). Previous studies reported that the main quantitative trait locus (QTL) for pepper resistance against <i>C. capsici</i> was located on chromosome 9 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Lee J, Do JW, Yoon JB. Development of STS markers linked to the major QTLs for resistance to the pepper anthracnose caused by Colletotrichum acutatum and C. capsici. Hortic Environ Biotechnol. 2011;52:596–601." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1825">39</a>]; however, we found that QTL is located on chromosome 3 due to translocation in Baccatum and Annuum (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig1">1c</a>). We obtained 35 Baccatum-specific NLRs (27 in CNL-G2, five in CNL-G10, and three in CNL-G10) from the 64 NLRs by sequence comparison among the three pepper genomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig5">5</a>). Considering the gene duplication history, 15 of the 35 genes appear to have emerged after generation of the Baccatum lineage and all of them belong to the CNL-G2 category. Transcriptome evidence indicated that ten of those 15 genes are expressed in one or more tissues (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig5">5</a>; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S23). Furthermore, five of the 15 genes appear to have emerged by retroduplication (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig5">5</a>). Consequently, our results suggest that the retroduplication along with tandem and segmental duplications, may have played a major role in the emergence of the candidate genes for anthracnose resistance in the Baccatum lineage.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-017-1341-9/MediaObjects/13059_2017_1341_Fig5_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-017-1341-9/MediaObjects/13059_2017_1341_Fig5_HTML.gif" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Potential anthracnose resistance genes in chromosome 3 of <i>C. baccatum</i>. Baccatum-specific NLRs in the major QTL region are visualized on 3.8 Mb of chromosome 3. The chromosome <i>plot</i> shows the subgroups, proposed retroduplication events, and expression results for the NLRs. The <i>black</i> and <i>red texts</i> indicate NLR IDs that emerged before and after the speciation of Baccatum, respectively</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>In this study, we generated new and improved genome resources for three <i>Capsicum</i> species. Our data provide an accurate and updated gene model of the pre-existing reference pepper genome based on annotation with accumulated knowledge, highlighting the importance of genome improvement after the completion of sequencing project. High-quality chromosome-scale pseudomolecules constructed from three pepper genomes enabled precise comparisons of genome structures and evolutionary analyses, providing new insights into interspecies diversification via genome rearrangements and lineage-specific evolution of LTR-Rs and genes in the genus <i>Capsicum</i>. Furthermore, we found evidences of the evolution of NLRs by LTR-mediated retroduplication in dicot Solanaceae and monocot rice, suggesting that such phenomena are ubiquitous in angiosperms. Our results suggested that at least 5–18% of plant NLRs were emerged by LTR-R-driven retroduplication (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S13). A notable feature of this retroduplication is that distinct subfamilies of NLRs were highly retroduplicated in different plant lineages. We unveiled the emergence of functional disease resistance genes in the Solanaceae family by retroduplication and the subsequent neofunctionalization of those genes by dynamic evolutionary processes including lineage-specific sequence mutation and tandem duplication. Our data suggest that a large proportion of all genes (~4–10%) in plant species might have emerged by LTR-R-mediated duplication. We observed the lineage-specific amplification of specific gene families by LTR-Rs in various plant species, including such genes as those encoding cytochrome P450s in potato and MADS-box TFs in Baccatum (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S18). Taken together, our results provide new insights into the evolution of functional plant disease-resistance genes that belong to the NLR family as well as other high copy number gene families that are present in higher plants.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Genome assembly</h3><p>In total, 425.7 Gb (132.2X coverage) and 526.7 Gb (136.1X coverage) of the Chinense and Baccatum genome sequences were generated in the Illumina Hiseq 2500 system (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S1). To remove unnecessary sequences for genome assembly, preprocessing analysis was performed as previously described [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Kim S, Park M, Yeom SI, Kim YM, Lee JM, Lee HA, et al. Genome sequence of the hot pepper provides insights into the evolution of pungency in Capsicum species. Nat Genet. 2014;46:270–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1911">25</a>] (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S2). The de novo genome assembly of each species was performed with SOAPdenovo2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Luo R, Liu B, Xie Y, Li Z, Huang W, Yuan J, et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience. 2012;1:18." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1917">40</a>] using the filtered raw sequences with parameters K = 77 and K = 81 for Chinense and Baccatum, respectively (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S3). The SSPACE software [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Boetzer M, Henkel CV, Jansen HJ, Butler D, Pirovano W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics. 2011;27:578–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1924">41</a>] was employed for additional scaffolding (-x 0 -m 46 -k 10 -a 0.4 -p 1); Gapcloser v.1.12 (GapCloser v112, <a href="http://soap.genomics.org.cn/down/GapCloser_release_2011.tar.gz">http://soap.genomics.org.cn/down/GapCloser_release_2011.tar.gz</a>) and Platanus [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Kajitani R, Toshimoto K, Noguchi H, Toyoda A, Ogura Y, Okuno M, et al. Efficient de novo assembly of highly heterozygous genomes from whole-genome shotgun short reads. Genome Res. 2014;24:1384–95." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1934">42</a>] were implemented using default parameters to close gaps.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Gene and repeat annotations</h3><p>Gene annotation was performed for the three pepper genomes as described in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a> (Figure S2). To annotate protein-coding genes, we assembled transcripts using Tophat and Cufflinks [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Ghosh S, Chan CK. Analysis of RNA-Seq data using TopHat and Cufflinks. Methods Mol Biol. 2016;1374:339–61." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1948">43</a>] with the RNA-seq reads described in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a> (Table S7) and in a previous study [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Kim S, Park M, Yeom SI, Kim YM, Lee JM, Lee HA, et al. Genome sequence of the hot pepper provides insights into the evolution of pungency in Capsicum species. Nat Genet. 2014;46:270–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1954">25</a>]. The ISGAP pipeline [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Kim S, Kim MS, Kim YM, Yeom SI, Cheong K, Kim KT, et al. Integrative structural annotation of de novo RNA-Seq provides an accurate reference gene set of the enormous genome of the onion (Allium cepa L.). DNA Res. 2015;22:19–27." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1957">44</a>] was used to extract accurate coding sequences from the assembled transcripts. Plant refSeq [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Pruitt KD, Tatusova T, Brown GR, Maglott DR. NCBI Reference Sequences (RefSeq): current status, new features and genome annotation policy. Nucleic Acids Res. 2012;40:D130–5." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1961">45</a>] and the public protein databases for <i>Arabidopsis</i> (TAIR 10), tomato (iTAG 2.3), potato (PGSC v3.4), and pepper (PGA v1.55) were used with Exonerate v2.2.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Slater GS, Birney E. Automated generation of heuristics for biological sequence comparison. BMC Bioinformatics. 2005;6:31." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1967">46</a>] to align protein to the pepper genomes. Ab initio prediction was carried out with AUGUSTUS [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Stanke M, Tzvetkova A, Morgenstern B. AUGUSTUS at EGASP: using EST, protein and genomic alignments for improved gene prediction in the human genome. Genome Biol. 2006;7:S11–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1970">47</a>] version 3.0 using an in-house training set consisting of full-length complementary DNA generated from transcriptome analysis and by protein alignment. Consensus gene models were determined with EVM [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Haas BJ, Salzberg SL, Zhu W, Pertea M, Allen JE, Orvis J, et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the program to assemble spliced alignments. Genome Biol. 2008;9:R7." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1973">48</a>] and the biological description of each gene model was assigned based on the Uniprot database and INTERPRO scan v5.15-54.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="McDowall J, Hunter S. InterPro protein classification. Methods Mol Biol. 2011;694:37–47." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1976">49</a>].</p><p>Repeat sequences were annotated in the initial contigs representing the estimated genome sizes and the assembled genomes of the three peppers, as shown in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S4. An integrated repeat library of the three peppers was constructed using RepeatModeler (RepeatModeler, <a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler/</a>). Annotation of intact LTR-Rs was performed using LTRHarvest [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Ellinghaus D, Kurtz S, Willhoeft U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinformatics. 2008;9:18." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1992">50</a>] (-maxlenltr 2000 and –similar 80) and LTRDigest [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Steinbiss S, Willhoeft U, Gremme G, Kurtz S. Fine-grained annotation and classification of de novo predicted LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 2009;37:7002–13." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d9587e1995">51</a>]. The subgroup of LTR-Rs in the integrated library was classified by comparing their sequences to those of the intact LTR-Rs using BLASTN (similarity &gt; 90%) (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S4).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Comparison of genome structures</h3><p>To identify regions that were either conserved or translocated between the <i>Capsicum</i> and <i>Solanum</i> species, we performed collinear analysis with MCScanX [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Wang Y, Tang H, Debarry JD, Tan X, Li J, Wang X, et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 2012;40:e49." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d9587e2015">52</a>] using the gene models of the three peppers and the tomato and potato genomes described in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a> (Table S11). We identified regions that were not translocated between the tomato and potato genomes as conserved blocks in the <i>Solanum</i> species.
