The conserved blocks in the <i>Solanum</i> species were then compared to the three pepper genomes. Blocks in the pepper genomes that were conserved or translocated between the <i>Capsicum</i> and <i>Solanum</i> species were determined as shown in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a> (Figure S5). To investigate the translocated blocks in the three pepper genomes, we examined the gene collinearity for syntenic blocks as shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig1">1c</a> and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a> (Figure S5).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Gene duplication history</h3><p>To estimate the gene duplication times of the annotated genes in the pepper genomes, we constructed a computational pipeline by modifying a previously described method [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Kim SB, Kang WH, Huy HN, Yeom SI, An JT, Kim S, et al. Divergent evolution of multiple virus-resistance genes from a progenitor in Capsicum spp. New Phytol. 2017;213:886–99." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d9587e2051">53</a>]. We first performed gene clustering analysis using OrthoMCL [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Li L, Stoeckert Jr CJ, Roos DS. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 2003;13:2178–89." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d9587e2054">54</a>] to classify the gene family. We assumed that the genes in the same clusters were in the same family and performed all-by-all alignments of the coding sequences within the clusters in each species using PRANK [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Loytynoja A. Phylogeny-aware alignment with PRANK. Methods Mol Biol. 2014;1079:155–70." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d9587e2057">55</a>]. For each alignment result, the <i>Ks</i> values were calculated using KaKs Calculator [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Zhang Z, Li J, Zhao XQ, Wang J, Wong GK, Yu J. KaKs_Calculator: calculating Ka and Ks through model selection and model averaging. Genomics Proteomics Bioinformatics. 2006;4:259–63." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d9587e2063">56</a>] and single-linkage clustering for the <i>Ks</i> values was performed using the hclust function in the R package. The molecular clock rate (<i>r</i>) was calculated to be 6.96 × 10<sup>−9</sup> substitutions per synonymous site per year [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="de Sa Moniz M, Drouin G. Phylogeny and substitution rates of angiosperm actin genes. Mol Biol Evol. 1996;13:1198–212." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d9587e2075">57</a>]. The duplication time was estimated using the formula, <i>Ks</i> value/2r.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Estimation of divergence time</h3><p>To estimate the divergence times of the plant genomes, we identified 2540 single copy genes in the rice, <i>Arabidopsis</i> (TAIR10), grape (VvGDB v2.0), tomato (v2.3), and potato (PGSC v3.4) genomes and the three pepper genomes using OrthoMCL clustering [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Li L, Stoeckert Jr CJ, Roos DS. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 2003;13:2178–89." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d9587e2093">54</a>] (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S11). Multiple alignments of the single copy genes from the eight genomes were implemented using PRANK [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Loytynoja A. Phylogeny-aware alignment with PRANK. Methods Mol Biol. 2014;1079:155–70." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d9587e2099">55</a>] (-f = nexus -codon). The speciation times of the eight plant species were calculated by phylogenetic analysis using the BEAST package [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Drummond AJ, Suchard MA, Xie D, Rambaut A. Bayesian phylogenetics with BEAUti and the BEAST 1.7. Mol Biol Evol. 2012;29:1969–73." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d9587e2102">58</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Evolutionary analyses of LTR-Rs</h3><p>For the intact LTR-Rs, we performed alignment of the sequences between the 5′ and 3′ LTRs using PRANK. The DNA substitution rates (<i>K</i>) between the 5′ and 3′ LTRs were calculated using baseml in the PAML package [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Yang Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol Biol Evol. 2007;24:1586–91." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d9587e2116">59</a>]. The insertion times of the LTR-Rs were estimated using the formula, <i>K/2r</i> (<i>r</i> =1.3 × 10<sup>−8</sup>) considering higher substitution rate in intergenic regions than that in coding regions [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Ma J, Bennetzen JL. Rapid recent growth and divergence of rice nuclear genomes. Proc Natl Acad Sci. 2004;101:12404–10." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d9587e2128">60</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Identification of retroduplicated NLRs in the plant genomes</h3><p>To identify NLR genes inside LTR-Rs, we used the rice (MSU RGAP 7), potato (PGSC v3.4), and tomato (v2.3) genomes with the three pepper genomes. We first identified NLRs using a previously constructed pipeline [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Seo E, Kim S, Yeom SI, Choi D. Genome-wide comparative analyses reveal the dynamic evolution of nucleotide-binding leucine-rich repeat gene family among Solanaceae Plants. Front Plant Sci. 2016;7:1205." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d9587e2139">21</a>] and extracted the NLRs within putative LTR-Rs predicted by LTRHarvest (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig3">3a</a>; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S13). We then compared those results with the repeats annotated by RepeatMasker and if the NLRs inside LTR-Rs overlapped with other TEs such as DNA transposons or Helitrons, we considered the LTR-Rs predicted by LTRHarvest to be incorrect and removed them. Because of rapid deletion of LTR-Rs and other unselected DNA in all flowering plants [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Ma J, Devos KM, Bennetzen JL. Analyses of LTR-retrotransposon structures reveal recent and rapid genomic DNA loss in rice. Genome Res. 2004;14:860–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d9587e2148">14</a>], we performed an additional identification of NLRs inside LTR-Rs using the annotated repeats including the partial LTR-Rs generated by RepeatMasker. We reasoned that if the NLRs were fully contained within LTR-Rs annotated by RepeatMasker, the NLRs were retroduplicated. To verify intron removal from the retroduplicated NLRs, we determined whether the candidate parental sequences of the NLRs contained multiple exons and had increased exon numbers by aligning the candidate parental sequences with the NLRs using Exonerate [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Slater GS, Birney E. Automated generation of heuristics for biological sequence comparison. BMC Bioinformatics. 2005;6:31." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d9587e2151">46</a>], requiring &gt; 95% query coverage of the NLRs. To increase analysis accuracy, we excluded unclear cases where multi-exon NLRs having no parental sequences were detected inside LTR-Rs.To predict whole genes inside LTR-Rs in the six plant genomes, we performed genome-wide identification of possible structure of LTR-Rs using LTRHarvest, taking into account rapid sequence change between the LTRs (--similar 75%, minltrlen 100). Like annotation of the NLRs inside LTR-Rs, we extracted genes within directly repeated LTR regions as putatively retroduplicated genes. For the genes inside LTR-Rs, the number of expressed genes in one or more tissues was counted using RNA-seq data, as described in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S7 and in previous analyses [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Kim S, Park M, Yeom SI, Kim YM, Lee JM, Lee HA, et al. Genome sequence of the hot pepper provides insights into the evolution of pungency in Capsicum species. Nat Genet. 2014;46:270–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d9587e2158">25</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature. 2011;475:189–95." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d9587e2161">35</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="The Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature. 2012;485:635–41." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d9587e2164">61</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Goff SA, Ricke D, Lan TH, Presting G, Wang R, Dunn M, et al. A draft sequence of the rice genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp. japonica). Science. 2002;296:92–100." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d9587e2167">62</a>] (See Rice gene expression data, <a href="http://rice.plantbiology.msu.edu/">http://rice.plantbiology.msu.edu/</a> and Potato gene expression data, <a href="http://solanaceae.plantbiology.msu.edu/pgsc_download.shtml">http://solanaceae.plantbiology.msu.edu/pgsc_download.shtml</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Identification of false-negative and false-positive retroduplicated NLRs</h3><p>We classified normal NLRs as false-negative annotations. From the total NLRs, we first excluded pre-identified retroNLRs. Of the remaining NLRs, the NLRs overlapped to LTR-regions (&gt; 80% coverage) predicted by RepeatMasker were excluded. Finally, the remaining NLRs were considered as normal NLRs (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S13). To remove false-positive annotations among retroduplicated NLRs, we analyzed structure of the retroNLRs inside LTR-Rs in the six plant genomes. Because we detected cases which two or three genes were inside single LTR-Rs, we determined the criteria for accurate discrimination of retrogenes among the multiple genes inside single LTR-Rs (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S6). We first excluded pre-annotated retroNLRs having no parental sequences then compared exon length and number of the remained genes. If the genes inside same LTR-R had the same exon number, we classified that a gene having the longest exon as a retrogene candidate (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S6a). In case of genes having different exon numbers inside a single LTR-R, we selected the longest exon-containing gene (if the exon is &gt; 1.5-fold larger than all of the exons in neighboring genes) as a retrogene candidate (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S6b, c). If not, we selected the gene having the smallest exon number as a retrogene candidate (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S6d). After manual confirmation, we filtered out 148 (25%) of 591 pre-identified retroNLR candidates in the six genomes. Finally, we determined 105, 123, 86, 21, 81, and 27 retroNLRs in Annuum, Baccatum, Chinense, tomato, potato, and rice genomes (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S7, S10, and Table S13).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Evolutionary investigation of functional disease-resistance genes in Solanaceae genomes</h3><p>The <i>L</i>, <i>I2</i>, and <i>R3a</i> genes of pepper, tomato, and potato were used to investigate evolutionary processes acting on functional disease-resistance genes in the Solanaceae plants. The <i>L</i> genes in the <i>Capsicum</i> spp. were aligned to paralogues in the pepper genomes using Exonerate [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Slater GS, Birney E. Automated generation of heuristics for biological sequence comparison. BMC Bioinformatics. 2005;6:31." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d9587e2236">46</a>] and closest homologs were identified (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S20). All of the closest homologs in each species were found to contain multiple exons and a gene that we named P1 was identified as the most likely parental sequence. By comparison of the sequence divergence between P1 and its closest homologs in the other genomes, we confirmed that P1 was Annuum-specific (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S11c). The 5′ and 3′ UTRs of <i>L1a</i> annotated based on RNA-seq evidence were also compared to the UTRs of P1 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig4">4</a>).</p><p>For <i>R3a</i> in the potato, we aligned the coding sequences of <i>R3a</i> and genes within its cluster downloaded from GenBank (AY849382, AY849383, AY849384, and AY849385) to the potato genome. Because of the absence of those genes in the potato reference genome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature. 2011;475:189–95." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d9587e2260">35</a>], we identified the closest homologs of <i>R3a</i> except <i>R3a</i> and its clustered genes in the potato genome. The duplication time of the <i>R3a</i> family was estimated by comparison of <i>R3a</i> and its homologs identified in the potato genome with the clustered genes. The <i>I2</i> sequence of tomato downloaded from GenBank was also used to search in tomato reference genome, but <i>I2</i> was not found.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Identification of potential anthracnose resistance genes</h3><p>To obtain candidate anthracnose resistance genes for <i>C. capsici</i>, we extracted NLRs located in the terminal region of the short arm of chromosome 3 of Baccatum based on pre-existing genetic information [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Lee J, Do JW, Yoon JB. Development of STS markers linked to the major QTLs for resistance to the pepper anthracnose caused by Colletotrichum acutatum and C. capsici. Hortic Environ Biotechnol. 2011;52:596–601." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d9587e2293">39</a>] (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S23). Candidate genes that may provide resistance in Baccatum against <i>C. capsici</i> were determined based on the degree of sequence conservation and the gene duplication time (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9#Fig5">5</a>).</p></div></div></section>

                        <span class="c-article-identifiers"><a class="c-article-identifiers__item" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=search&amp;term=Doil+Choi" data-track="click" data-track-action="author link - pubmed" data-track-label="link">PubMed</a><span class="u-hide"> </span><a class="c-article-identifiers__item" href="http://scholar.google.co.uk/scholar?as_q=&amp;num=10&amp;btnG=Search+Scholar&amp;as_epq=&amp;as_oq=&amp;as_eq=&amp;as_occt=any&amp;as_sauthors=%22Doil+Choi%22&amp;as_publication=&amp;as_ylo=&amp;as_yhi=&amp;as_allsubj=all&amp;hl=en" data-track="click" data-track-action="author link - scholar" data-track-label="link">Google Scholar</a></span></p></div></div></li></ol></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="contributions">Contributions</h3><p>DC conceived the project, designed the content, and organized the manuscript. SK performed data generation/analysis and managed subprojects. S-BK, H-AL, and H-YL prepared the DNA and RNA samples. SK, KC, KH, and JL performed the <i>de novo</i> genome construction of two domesticated pepper genomes and the improvement of pre-existing reference genome. SK, M-SK, JP, Y-MK, NK, and RWK carried out the gene annotation. SK, JKL, S-CL, T-JY, JK, H-YL, and B-CK fulfilled the repeat annotation and analyses. SK, H-SS, JJ, JHH, and Y-HL implemented the genome structure comparison and evolutionary analyses of TEs and genes. SK, ES, JP, YL, SO, JHL, EC, EC, SEL, GC, HS, and W-HK performed the gene family analyses. SK, ES, and JP carried out the retroduplication analyses for the NLRs and other gene families. SK, HK, H-SS, and YH designed and visualized the figures. SK, S-IY, Y-MK, K-TK, J-ML, MP, JLB, and DC wrote the manuscript. All authors read and approved the final manuscript.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding author</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
                <a id="corresp-c1" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9/email/correspondent/c1/new">Doil Choi</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="ethics" data-title="Ethics declarations"><div class="c-article-section" id="ethics-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="ethics">Ethics declarations</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="ethics-content">
